<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Horse</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Domesticated four-footed mammal from the equine family</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Horse_(disambiguation)" title="Horse (disambiguation)">Horse (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>

<td colspan="2" style="text-align: left">

</p>
</td></tr>

<p>The <b>horse</b> (<i><b>Equus ferus caballus</b></i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSW3_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-MSW3-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> is a <a href="/wiki/Domestication" title="Domestication">domesticated</a>, <a href="/wiki/Odd-toed_ungulate" title="Odd-toed ungulate">one-toed</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ungulate" title="Ungulate">hoofed mammal</a>. It belongs to the taxonomic family <a href="/wiki/Equidae" title="Equidae">Equidae</a> and is one of two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extant_taxon" title="Extant taxon">extant</a> <a href="/wiki/Subspecies" title="Subspecies">subspecies</a> of <a href="/wiki/Wild_horse" title="Wild horse"><i>Equus ferus</i></a>. The horse has <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_the_horse" title="Evolution of the horse">evolved</a> over the past 45 to 55 million years from a small multi-toed creature, <i><a href="/wiki/Eohippus" title="Eohippus">Eohippus</a></i>, into the large, single-toed animal of today. Humans began domesticating horses around 4000 BC, and their <a href="/wiki/Domestication_of_the_horse" title="Domestication of the horse">domestication</a> is believed to have been widespread by 3000 BC. Horses in the subspecies <i>caballus</i> are domesticated, although some domesticated populations live in the wild as <a href="/wiki/Feral_horse" title="Feral horse">feral horses</a>. These feral populations are not true <a href="/wiki/Wild_horse" title="Wild horse">wild horses</a>, as this term is used to describe horses that have never been domesticated. There is an extensive, specialized vocabulary used to describe equine-related concepts, covering everything from <a href="/wiki/Equine_anatomy" title="Equine anatomy">anatomy</a> to life stages, size, <a href="/wiki/Equine_coat_color" title="Equine coat color">colors</a>, <a href="/wiki/Horse_markings" title="Horse markings">markings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Horse_breed" title="Horse breed">breeds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Horse_gait" title="Horse gait">locomotion</a> and behavior.
</p><p>Horses are <a href="/wiki/Cursorial" title="Cursorial">adapted to run</a>, allowing them to quickly escape predators, and possess an excellent <a href="/wiki/Sense_of_balance" title="Sense of balance">sense of balance</a> and a strong <a href="/wiki/Fight-or-flight_response" title="Fight-or-flight response">fight-or-flight response</a>. Related to this need to flee from predators in the wild is an unusual trait: horses are able to sleep both standing up and lying down, with younger horses tending to sleep significantly more than adults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Female horses, called <a href="/wiki/Mare" title="Mare">mares</a>, carry their young for approximately 11 months and a young horse, called a <a href="/wiki/Foal" title="Foal">foal</a>, can stand and run shortly following birth. Most domesticated horses begin training under a <a href="/wiki/Saddle" title="Saddle">saddle</a> or in a <a href="/wiki/Horse_harness" title="Horse harness">harness</a> between the ages of two and four. They reach full adult development by age five, and have an average lifespan of between 25 and 30 years.
</p><p>Horse breeds are loosely divided into three categories based on general temperament: spirited "hot bloods" with speed and endurance; "cold bloods", such as <a href="/wiki/Draft_horse" title="Draft horse">draft horses</a> and some <a href="/wiki/Pony" title="Pony">ponies</a>, suitable for slow, heavy work; and "<a href="/wiki/Warmblood" title="Warmblood">warmbloods</a>", developed from crosses between hot bloods and cold bloods, often focusing on creating breeds for specific riding purposes, particularly in Europe. There are more than 300 breeds of horse in the world today, developed for many different uses.
</p><p>Horses and humans interact in a wide variety of sport competitions and non-competitive recreational pursuits as well as in working activities such as <a href="/wiki/Mounted_police" title="Mounted police">police work</a>, <a href="/wiki/Working_animal" title="Working animal">agriculture</a>, entertainment and <a href="/wiki/Equine-assisted_therapy" title="Equine-assisted therapy">therapy</a>. Horses were historically used in warfare, from which a wide variety of <a href="/wiki/Equestrianism" title="Equestrianism">riding</a> and <a href="/wiki/Driving_(horse)" title="Driving (horse)">driving</a> techniques developed, using many different styles of <a href="/wiki/Horse_tack" title="Horse tack">equipment</a> and methods of control. Many products are derived from horses including meat, milk, hide, hair, bone and pharmaceuticals extracted from the urine of pregnant mares. Humans provide domesticated horses with food, water and shelter as well as attention from specialists such as <a href="/wiki/Veterinarian" title="Veterinarian">veterinarians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Farrier" title="Farrier">farriers</a>.
</p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-33"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-34"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-35"><a href="#Sources"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Sources</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-36"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-37"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biology">Biology</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Equine_anatomy" title="Equine anatomy">Equine anatomy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Points_of_a_horse.jpg"><img alt="Diagram of a horse with some parts labeled." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="538" data-file-width="819" decoding="async" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Points_of_a_horse.jpg/330px-Points_of_a_horse.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Points_of_a_horse.jpg/495px-Points_of_a_horse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Points_of_a_horse.jpg/660px-Points_of_a_horse.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Points_of_a_horse.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Points of a horse<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Specific terms and specialized language are used to describe <a href="/wiki/Equine_anatomy" title="Equine anatomy">equine anatomy</a>, different life stages, and colors and breeds.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lifespan_and_life_stages">Lifespan and life stages</span></h3>
<p>Depending on breed, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horse_care" title="Horse care">management</a> and environment, the modern domestic horse has a life expectancy of 25 to 30 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ensminger46_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ensminger46-7">[7]</a></sup> Uncommonly, a few animals live into their 40s and, occasionally, beyond.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> The oldest verifiable record was "<a href="/wiki/Old_Billy" title="Old Billy">Old Billy</a>", a 19th-century horse that lived to the age of 62.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ensminger46_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ensminger46-7">[7]</a></sup> In modern times, Sugar Puff, who had been listed in <i><a href="/wiki/Guinness_World_Records" title="Guinness World Records">Guinness World Records</a></i> as the world's oldest living pony, died in 2007 at age 56.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>Regardless of a horse or pony's actual birth date, for most competition purposes a year is added to its age each January 1 of each year in the Northern Hemisphere<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ensminger46_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ensminger46-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> and each August 1 in the Southern Hemisphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> The exception is in <a href="/wiki/Endurance_riding" title="Endurance riding">endurance riding</a>, where the minimum age to compete is based on the animal's actual calendar age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Endurance_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Endurance-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>The following terminology is used to describe horses of various ages:
</p>
<dl><dt><a href="/wiki/Foal" title="Foal">Foal</a></dt>
<dd>A horse of either sex less than one year old. A nursing foal is sometimes called a <i>suckling</i>, and a foal that has been weaned is called a <i>weanling</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ensminger418_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ensminger418-13">[13]</a></sup> Most domesticated foals are weaned at five to seven months of age, although foals can be weaned at four months with no adverse physical effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></dd>
<dt><a href="/wiki/Yearling_(horse)" title="Yearling (horse)">Yearling</a></dt>
<dd>A horse of either sex that is between one and two years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></dd>
<dt><a href="/wiki/Colt_(horse)" title="Colt (horse)">Colt</a></dt>
<dd>A male horse under the age of four.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> A common terminology error is to call any young horse a "colt", when the term actually only refers to young male horses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></dd>
<dt><a href="/wiki/Filly" title="Filly">Filly</a></dt>
<dd>A female horse under the age of four.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ensminger418_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ensminger418-13">[13]</a></sup></dd>
<dt><a href="/wiki/Mare" title="Mare">Mare</a></dt>
<dd>A female horse four years old and older.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></dd>
<dt><a href="/wiki/Stallion" title="Stallion">Stallion</a></dt>
<dd>A non-castrated male horse four years old and older.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> The term "horse" is sometimes used colloquially to refer specifically to a stallion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ensminger420_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ensminger420-20">[20]</a></sup></dd>
<dt><a href="/wiki/Gelding" title="Gelding">Gelding</a></dt>
<dd>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Castrated" title="Castrated">castrated</a> male horse of any age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ensminger418_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ensminger418-13">[13]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Horse_racing" title="Horse racing">horse racing</a>, these definitions may differ: For example, in the British Isles, <a href="/wiki/Thoroughbred" title="Thoroughbred">Thoroughbred</a> horse racing defines colts and fillies as less than five years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> However, Australian Thoroughbred racing defines colts and fillies as less than four years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Size_and_measurement">Size and measurement</span></h3>
<p>The height of horses is measured at the highest point of the <a href="/wiki/Withers" title="Withers">withers</a>, where the neck meets the <a href="/wiki/Back_(horse)" title="Back (horse)">back</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whitaker77_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Whitaker77-23">[23]</a></sup> This point is used because it is a stable point of the anatomy, unlike the head or neck, which move up and down in relation to the body of the horse.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Horse-and-pony.jpg"><img alt="A large brown horse is chasing a small horse in a pasture." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1365" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Horse-and-pony.jpg/310px-Horse-and-pony.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Horse-and-pony.jpg/465px-Horse-and-pony.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Horse-and-pony.jpg/620px-Horse-and-pony.jpg 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Horse-and-pony.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Size varies greatly among horse breeds, as with this full-sized horse and small pony.</div></div></div>
<p>In English-speaking countries, the height of horses is often stated in units of <a href="/wiki/Hand_(unit)" title="Hand (unit)">hands</a> and inches: one hand is equal to 4 inches (101.6 mm). The height is expressed as the number of full hands, followed by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radix_point" title="Radix point">point</a>, then the number of additional inches, and ending with the abbreviation "h" or "hh" (for "hands high"). Thus, a horse described as "15.2 h" is 15 hands plus 2 inches, for a total of 62 inches (157.5 cm) in height.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ensminger51_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ensminger51-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>The size of horses varies by breed, but also is influenced by <a href="/wiki/Equine_nutrition" title="Equine nutrition">nutrition</a>. Light riding horses usually range in height from 14 to 16 hands (56 to 64 inches, 142 to 163 cm) and can weigh from 380 to 550 kilograms (840 to 1,210 lb).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Larger riding horses usually start at about 15.2 hands (62 inches, 157 cm) and often are as tall as 17 hands (68 inches, 173 cm), weighing from 500 to 600 kilograms (1,100 to 1,320 lb).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Heavy or <a href="/wiki/Draft_horse" title="Draft horse">draft horses</a> are usually at least 16 hands (64 inches, 163 cm) high and can be as tall as 18 hands (72 inches, 183 cm) high. They can weigh from about 700 to 1,000 kilograms (1,540 to 2,200 lb).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>The largest horse in recorded history was probably a <a href="/wiki/Shire_horse" title="Shire horse">Shire horse</a> named <a href="/wiki/Sampson_(horse)" title="Sampson (horse)">Mammoth</a>, who was born in 1848. He stood <span class="frac nowrap">21.2<sup><span style="position:absolute; left:-10000px"> </span>1</sup>⁄<sub>4</sub></span> hands (86.25 inches, 219 cm) high and his peak weight was estimated at 1,524 kilograms (3,360 lb).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whitaker60_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Whitaker60-28">[28]</a></sup> The record holder for the smallest horse ever is <a href="/wiki/Thumbelina_(horse)" title="Thumbelina (horse)">Thumbelina</a>, a fully mature <a href="/wiki/Miniature_horse" title="Miniature horse">miniature horse</a> affected by <a href="/wiki/Dwarfism" title="Dwarfism">dwarfism</a>. She was 17 in (43 cm) tall and weighed 57 lb (26 kg).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ponies">Ponies</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pony" title="Pony">Pony</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pony" title="Pony">Ponies</a> are <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">taxonomically</a> the same animals as horses. The distinction between a horse and pony is commonly drawn on the basis of height, especially for competition purposes. However, height alone is not dispositive; the difference between horses and ponies may also include aspects of <a href="/wiki/Phenotype" title="Phenotype">phenotype</a>, including conformation and temperament.
</p><p>The traditional standard for height of a horse or a pony at maturity is 14.2 hands (58 inches, 147 cm). An animal 14.2 h or over is usually considered to be a horse and one less than 14.2 h a pony,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EnsmingerHT11_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-EnsmingerHT11-31">[31]</a></sup> but there are many exceptions to the traditional standard. In Australia, ponies are considered to be those under 14 hands (56 inches, 142 cm).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> For competition in the <a href="/wiki/Western_riding" title="Western riding">Western</a> division of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Equestrian_Federation" title="United States Equestrian Federation">United States Equestrian Federation</a>, the cutoff is 14.1 hands (57 inches, 145 cm).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/International_Federation_for_Equestrian_Sports" title="International Federation for Equestrian Sports">International Federation for Equestrian Sports</a>, the world governing body for horse sport, uses <a href="/wiki/Metric_system" title="Metric system">metric</a> measurements and defines a pony as being any horse measuring less than 148 centimetres (58.27 in) at the withers without shoes, which is just over 14.2 h, and 149 centimetres (58.66 in), or just over 14.2<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1020198016">.mw-parser-output .frac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .frac .num,.mw-parser-output .frac .den{font-size:80%;line-height:0;vertical-align:super}.mw-parser-output .frac .den{vertical-align:sub}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style><span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">1</span>⁄<span class="den">2</span></span> h, with shoes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>Height is not the sole criterion for distinguishing horses from ponies. <a href="/wiki/Breed_registry" title="Breed registry">Breed registries</a> for horses that typically produce individuals both under and over 14.2 h consider all animals of that breed to be horses regardless of their height.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Conversely, some pony breeds may have features in common with horses, and individual animals may occasionally mature at over 14.2 h, but are still considered to be ponies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ponies often exhibit thicker manes, tails, and overall coat. They also have proportionally shorter legs, wider barrels, heavier bone, shorter and thicker necks, and short heads with broad foreheads. They may have calmer temperaments than horses and also a high level of intelligence that may or may not be used to cooperate with human handlers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EnsmingerHT11_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-EnsmingerHT11-31">[31]</a></sup> Small size, by itself, is not an exclusive determinant. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Shetland_pony" title="Shetland pony">Shetland pony</a> which averages 10 hands (40 inches, 102 cm), is considered a pony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EnsmingerHT11_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-EnsmingerHT11-31">[31]</a></sup> Conversely, breeds such as the <a href="/wiki/Falabella" title="Falabella">Falabella</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Miniature_horse" title="Miniature horse">miniature horses</a>, which can be no taller than 30 inches (76 cm), are classified by their <a href="/wiki/Breed_registry" title="Breed registry">registries</a> as very small horses, not ponies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetics">Genetics</span></h3>
<p>Horses have 64 <a href="/wiki/Chromosome" title="Chromosome">chromosomes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Horse_genome" title="Horse genome">horse genome</a> was <a href="/wiki/DNA_sequencing" title="DNA sequencing">sequenced</a> in 2007. It contains 2.7 billion DNA <a href="/wiki/Base_pair" title="Base pair">base pairs</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cornell_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cornell-39">[39]</a></sup> which is larger than the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dog_genome" title="Dog genome">dog genome</a>, but smaller than the <a href="/wiki/Human_genome" title="Human genome">human genome</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Bovine_genome" title="Bovine genome">bovine genome</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ScienceDaily_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-ScienceDaily-40">[40]</a></sup> The map is available to researchers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colors_and_markings">Colors and markings</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Horsescd1l-095.jpg"><img alt="Two horses in a field. The one on the left is a dark brown with a black mane and tail. The one on the right is a light red all over." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="344" data-file-width="524" decoding="async" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Horsescd1l-095.jpg/310px-Horsescd1l-095.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Horsescd1l-095.jpg/465px-Horsescd1l-095.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/24/Horsescd1l-095.jpg 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Horsescd1l-095.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bay_(horse)" title="Bay (horse)">Bay</a> (left) and <a href="/wiki/Chestnut_(horse_color)" title="Chestnut (horse color)">chestnut</a> (sometimes called "sorrel") are two of the most common coat colors, seen in almost all breeds.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Equine_coat_color" title="Equine coat color">Equine coat color</a>, <a href="/wiki/Equine_coat_color_genetics" title="Equine coat color genetics">Equine coat color genetics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Horse_markings" title="Horse markings">Horse markings</a></div>
<p>Horses exhibit a diverse array of <a href="/wiki/Equine_coat_color" title="Equine coat color">coat colors</a> and distinctive <a href="/wiki/Horse_markings" title="Horse markings">markings</a>, described by a specialized vocabulary. Often, a horse is classified first by its coat color, before breed or sex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Horses of the same color may be distinguished from one another by white <a href="/wiki/Horse_markings" title="Horse markings">markings</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> which, along with various spotting patterns, are inherited separately from coat color.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many <a href="/wiki/Equine_coat_color_genetics" title="Equine coat color genetics">genes</a> that create horse coat colors and patterns have been identified. Current genetic tests can identify at least 13 different <a href="/wiki/Allele" title="Allele">alleles</a> influencing coat color,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UCVGL_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-UCVGL-45">[45]</a></sup> and research continues to discover new genes linked to specific traits. The basic coat colors of <a href="/wiki/Chestnut_(horse_color)" title="Chestnut (horse color)">chestnut</a> and <a href="/wiki/Black_horse" title="Black horse">black</a> are determined by the <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">gene</a> controlled by the <a href="/wiki/Melanocortin_1_receptor" title="Melanocortin 1 receptor">Melanocortin 1 receptor</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marklund1996_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marklund1996-46">[46]</a></sup> also known as the "extension gene" or "red factor,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UCVGL_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-UCVGL-45">[45]</a></sup> as its recessive form is "red" (chestnut) and its dominant form is black.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UCDIntro_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-UCDIntro-47">[47]</a></sup> Additional <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a> control suppression of black color to <a href="/wiki/Point_coloration" title="Point coloration">point coloration</a> that results in a <a href="/wiki/Bay_(horse)" title="Bay (horse)">bay</a>, spotting patterns such as <a href="/wiki/Pinto_horse" title="Pinto horse">pinto</a> or <a href="/wiki/Leopard_complex" title="Leopard complex">leopard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dilution_gene" title="Dilution gene">dilution genes</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Palomino" title="Palomino">palomino</a> or <a href="/wiki/Dun_gene" title="Dun gene">dun</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Gray_horse" title="Gray horse">graying</a>, and all the other factors that create the many possible coat colors found in horses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UCVGL_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-UCVGL-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>Horses that have a white coat color are often mislabeled; a horse that looks "white" is usually a middle-aged or older <a href="/wiki/Gray_horse" title="Gray horse">gray</a>. Grays are born a darker shade, get lighter as they age, but usually keep black skin underneath their white hair coat (with the exception of pink skin under white <a href="/wiki/Horse_markings" title="Horse markings">markings</a>). The only horses properly called <a href="/wiki/White_horse" title="White horse">white</a> are born with a predominantly white hair coat and pink skin, a fairly rare occurrence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UCDIntro_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-UCDIntro-47">[47]</a></sup> Different and unrelated <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetic</a> factors can produce white coat colors in horses, including several different alleles of <a href="/wiki/Dominant_white" title="Dominant white">dominant white</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sabino_horse" title="Sabino horse">sabino-1 gene</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-haase2009-similar_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-haase2009-similar-48">[48]</a></sup> However, there are no "<a href="/wiki/Albinism" title="Albinism">albino</a>" horses, defined as having both pink skin and red eyes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Duplicatetest_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Duplicatetest-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproduction_and_development">Reproduction and development</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Horse_breeding" title="Horse breeding">Horse breeding</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mare_foal_poland.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="4608" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Mare_foal_poland.jpg/220px-Mare_foal_poland.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Mare_foal_poland.jpg/330px-Mare_foal_poland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Mare_foal_poland.jpg/440px-Mare_foal_poland.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mare_foal_poland.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mare with a foal</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Gestation" title="Gestation">Gestation</a> lasts approximately 340 days, with an average range 320–370 days,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ensminger156_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ensminger156-50">[50]</a></sup> and usually results in one <a href="/wiki/Foal" title="Foal">foal</a>; twins are rare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Horses are a precocial species, and foals are capable of standing and running within a short time following birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Foals are usually born in the spring. The <a href="/wiki/Estrous_cycle" title="Estrous cycle">estrous cycle</a> of a mare occurs roughly every 19–22 days and occurs from early spring into autumn. Most mares enter an <i>anestrus</i> period during the winter and thus do not cycle in this period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ensminger150_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ensminger150-53">[53]</a></sup> Foals are generally <a href="/wiki/Weaning" title="Weaning">weaned</a> from their mothers between four and six months of age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>Horses, particularly colts, are sometimes physically capable of reproduction at about 18 months, but domesticated horses are rarely allowed to breed before the age of three, especially females.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HorseTack129_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-HorseTack129-55">[55]</a></sup> Horses four years old are considered mature, although the skeleton normally continues to develop until the age of six; maturation also depends on the horse's size, breed, sex, and quality of care. Larger horses have larger bones; therefore, not only do the bones take longer to form <a href="/wiki/Bone" title="Bone">bone tissue</a>, but the <a href="/wiki/Epiphyseal_plate" title="Epiphyseal plate">epiphyseal plates</a> are larger and take longer to convert from <a href="/wiki/Cartilage" title="Cartilage">cartilage</a> to bone. These plates convert after the other parts of the bones, and are crucial to development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>Depending on maturity, breed, and work expected, horses are usually put under saddle and <a href="/wiki/Horse_training" title="Horse training">trained</a> to be ridden between the ages of two and four.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Train163_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Train163-57">[57]</a></sup> Although <a href="/wiki/Thoroughbred" title="Thoroughbred">Thoroughbred</a> <a href="/wiki/Horse_racing" title="Horse racing">race horses</a> are put on the track as young as the age of two in some countries,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> horses specifically bred for sports such as <a href="/wiki/Dressage" title="Dressage">dressage</a> are generally not put under saddle until they are three or four years old, because their bones and muscles are not solidly developed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> For <a href="/wiki/Endurance_riding" title="Endurance riding">endurance riding</a> competition, horses are not deemed mature enough to compete until they are a full 60 calendar months (five years) old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Endurance_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Endurance-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anatomy">Anatomy</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Equine_anatomy" title="Equine anatomy">Equine anatomy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muscular_system_of_the_horse" title="Muscular system of the horse">Muscular system of the horse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_system_of_the_horse" title="Respiratory system of the horse">Respiratory system of the horse</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Circulatory_system_of_the_horse" title="Circulatory system of the horse">Circulatory system of the horse</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Skeletal_system">Skeletal system</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Skeletal_system_of_the_horse" title="Skeletal system of the horse">Skeletal system of the horse</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Horse_anatomy.svg"><img alt="Diagram of a horse skeleton with major parts labeled." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="402" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="243" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Horse_anatomy.svg/310px-Horse_anatomy.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Horse_anatomy.svg/465px-Horse_anatomy.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Horse_anatomy.svg/620px-Horse_anatomy.svg.png 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Horse_anatomy.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The skeletal system of a modern horse</div></div></div>
<p>The horse skeleton averages 205 bones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Evans90_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Evans90-60">[60]</a></sup> A significant difference between the horse skeleton and that of a human is the lack of a <a href="/wiki/Clavicle" title="Clavicle">collarbone</a>—the horse's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Equine_forelimb_anatomy" title="Equine forelimb anatomy">forelimbs</a> are attached to the <a href="/wiki/Vertebral_column" title="Vertebral column">spinal column</a> by a powerful set of muscles, tendons, and ligaments that attach the <a href="/wiki/Scapula" title="Scapula">shoulder blade</a> to the torso. The horse's four legs and hooves are also unique structures. Their leg bones are proportioned differently from those of a human. For example, the body part that is called a horse's "knee" is actually made up of the <a href="/wiki/Carpal_bones" title="Carpal bones">carpal bones</a> that correspond to the human <a href="/wiki/Wrist" title="Wrist">wrist</a>. Similarly, the <a href="/wiki/Hock_(anatomy)" title="Hock (anatomy)">hock</a> contains bones equivalent to those in the human <a href="/wiki/Ankle" title="Ankle">ankle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heel" title="Heel">heel</a>. The lower leg bones of a horse correspond to the bones of the human hand or foot, and the <a href="/wiki/Equine_anatomy" title="Equine anatomy">fetlock</a> (incorrectly called the "ankle") is actually the proximal <a href="/wiki/Sesamoid_bone" title="Sesamoid bone">sesamoid bones</a> between the <a href="/wiki/Equine_anatomy" title="Equine anatomy">cannon</a> bones (a single equivalent to the human <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metacarpus" title="Metacarpus">metacarpal</a> or <a href="/wiki/Metatarsal_bones" title="Metatarsal bones">metatarsal bones</a>) and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proximal_phalanges" title="Proximal phalanges">proximal phalanges</a>, located where one finds the "knuckles" of a human. A horse also has no muscles in its legs below the knees and hocks, only skin, hair, bone, <a href="/wiki/Tendon" title="Tendon">tendons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ligament" title="Ligament">ligaments</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cartilage" title="Cartilage">cartilage</a>, and the assorted specialized tissues that make up the <a href="/wiki/Horse_hoof" title="Horse hoof">hoof</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ensminger21_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ensminger21-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hooves">Hooves</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Horse_hoof" title="Horse hoof">Horse hoof</a>, <a href="/wiki/Horseshoe" title="Horseshoe">Horseshoe</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Farrier" title="Farrier">Farrier</a></div>
<p>The critical importance of the feet and legs is summed up by the traditional adage, "no foot, no horse".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ensminger367_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ensminger367-62">[62]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Horse_hoof" title="Horse hoof">horse hoof</a> begins with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distal_phalanges" title="Distal phalanges">distal phalanges</a>, the equivalent of the human fingertip or tip of the toe, surrounded by <a href="/wiki/Cartilage" title="Cartilage">cartilage</a> and other specialized, blood-rich soft tissues such as the <a href="/wiki/Horse_hoof#Internal_structures" title="Horse hoof">laminae</a>. The exterior hoof wall and horn of the sole is made of <a href="/wiki/Keratin" title="Keratin">keratin</a>, the same material as a human <a href="/wiki/Nail_(anatomy)" title="Nail (anatomy)">fingernail</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> The end result is that a horse, weighing on average 500 kilograms (1,100 lb),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> travels on the same bones as would a human on tiptoe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> For the protection of the hoof under certain conditions, some horses have <a href="/wiki/Horseshoe" title="Horseshoe">horseshoes</a> placed on their feet by a professional <a href="/wiki/Farrier" title="Farrier">farrier</a>. The hoof continually grows, and in most domesticated horses needs to be trimmed (and horseshoes reset, if used) every five to eight weeks,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> though the hooves of horses in the wild wear down and regrow at a rate suitable for their terrain.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Teeth">Teeth</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Horse_teeth" title="Horse teeth">Horse teeth</a></div>
<p>Horses are adapted to <a href="/wiki/Grazing" title="Grazing">grazing</a>. In an adult horse, there are 12 <a href="/wiki/Incisor" title="Incisor">incisors</a> at the front of the mouth, adapted to biting off the grass or other vegetation. There are 24 teeth adapted for chewing, the <a href="/wiki/Premolar" title="Premolar">premolars</a> and <a href="/wiki/Molar_(tooth)" title="Molar (tooth)">molars</a>, at the back of the mouth. Stallions and geldings have four additional teeth just behind the incisors, a type of <a href="/wiki/Canine_tooth" title="Canine tooth">canine teeth</a> called "tushes". Some horses, both male and female, will also develop one to four very small <a href="/wiki/Vestigiality" title="Vestigiality">vestigial</a> teeth in front of the molars, known as "wolf" teeth, which are generally removed because they can interfere with the <a href="/wiki/Bit_(horse)" title="Bit (horse)">bit</a>. There is an empty interdental space between the incisors and the molars where the bit rests directly on the gums, or "bars" of the horse's mouth when the horse is <a href="/wiki/Bridle" title="Bridle">bridled</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>An estimate of a horse's age can be made from looking at its teeth. The teeth continue to erupt throughout life and are worn down by grazing. Therefore, the incisors show changes as the horse ages; they develop a distinct wear pattern, changes in tooth shape, and changes in the angle at which the chewing surfaces meet. This allows a very rough estimate of a horse's age, although diet and veterinary care can also affect the rate of tooth wear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ensminger46_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-Ensminger46-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Digestion">Digestion</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Equine_anatomy#Digestive_system" title="Equine anatomy">Equine digestive system</a> and <a href="/wiki/Equine_nutrition" title="Equine nutrition">Equine nutrition</a></div>
<p>Horses are <a href="/wiki/Herbivore" title="Herbivore">herbivores</a> with a digestive system adapted to a <a href="/wiki/Forage" title="Forage">forage</a> diet of grasses and other plant material, consumed steadily throughout the day. Therefore, compared to humans, they have a relatively small stomach but very long intestines to facilitate a steady flow of nutrients. A 450-kilogram (990 lb) horse will eat 7 to 11 kilograms (15 to 24 lb) of food per day and, under normal use, drink 38 to 45 litres (8.4 to 9.9 imp gal; 10 to 12 US gal) of <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>. Horses are not <a href="/wiki/Ruminant" title="Ruminant">ruminants</a>, they have only one stomach, like humans, but unlike humans, they can utilize <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a>, a major component of grass. Horses are <a href="/wiki/Hindgut_fermentation" title="Hindgut fermentation">hindgut fermenters</a>. Cellulose fermentation by symbiotic bacteria occurs in the <a href="/wiki/Cecum" title="Cecum">cecum</a>, or "water gut", which food goes through before reaching the <a href="/wiki/Large_intestine" title="Large intestine">large intestine</a>. Horses cannot <a href="/wiki/Vomiting" title="Vomiting">vomit</a>, so digestion problems can quickly cause <a href="/wiki/Horse_colic" title="Horse colic">colic</a>, a leading cause of death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Horses do not have a <a href="/wiki/Gallbladder" title="Gallbladder">gallbladder</a>; however, they seem to tolerate high amounts of fat in their diet despite lack of a gallbladder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Senses">Senses</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pferdeauge.jpg"><img alt="Close up of a horse eye, which is dark brown with lashes on the top eyelid" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="901" data-file-width="1352" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Pferdeauge.jpg/220px-Pferdeauge.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Pferdeauge.jpg/330px-Pferdeauge.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Pferdeauge.jpg/440px-Pferdeauge.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pferdeauge.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A horse's eye</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Equine_vision" title="Equine vision">Equine vision</a></div>
<p>The horses' senses are based on their status as <a href="/wiki/Predation" title="Predation">prey animals</a>, where they must be aware of their surroundings at all times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ensminger309_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ensminger309-71">[71]</a></sup> They have the largest eyes of any land mammal,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sellnow_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sellnow-72">[72]</a></sup> and are lateral-eyed, meaning that their eyes are positioned on the sides of their heads.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> This means that horses have a range of vision of more than 350°, with approximately 65° of this being <a href="/wiki/Binocular_vision" title="Binocular vision">binocular vision</a> and the remaining 285° <a href="/wiki/Monocular_vision" title="Monocular vision">monocular vision</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sellnow_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sellnow-72">[72]</a></sup> Horses have excellent day and <a href="/wiki/Night_vision" title="Night vision">night vision</a>, but they have two-color, or <a href="/wiki/Dichromacy" title="Dichromacy">dichromatic vision</a>; their <a href="/wiki/Color_vision" title="Color vision">color vision</a> is somewhat like <a href="/wiki/Color_blindness" title="Color blindness">red-green color blindness</a> in humans, where certain colors, especially red and related colors, appear as a shade of green.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>Their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olfaction" title="Olfaction">sense of smell</a>, while much better than that of humans, is not quite as good as that of a dog. It is believed to play a key role in the social interactions of horses as well as detecting other key scents in the environment. Horses have two olfactory centers. The first system is in the nostrils and nasal cavity, which analyze a wide range of odors. The second, located under the nasal cavity, are the <a href="/wiki/Vomeronasal_organ" title="Vomeronasal organ">Vomeronasal organs</a>, also called Jacobson's organs. These have a separate nerve pathway to the brain and appear to primarily analyze <a href="/wiki/Pheromone" title="Pheromone">pheromones</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Briggs_smell_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Briggs_smell-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>A horse's hearing is good,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ensminger309_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ensminger309-71">[71]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Auricle_(anatomy)" title="Auricle (anatomy)">pinna</a> of each ear can rotate up to 180°, giving the potential for 360° hearing without having to move the head.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Noise impacts the behavior of horses and certain kinds of noise may contribute to stress: A 2013 study in the UK indicated that stabled horses were calmest in a quiet setting, or if listening to country or classical music, but displayed signs of nervousness when listening to jazz or rock music. This study also recommended keeping music under a volume of 21 <a href="/wiki/Decibel" title="Decibel">decibels</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Music_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Music-77">[77]</a></sup> An Australian study found that stabled racehorses listening to talk radio had a higher rate of gastric ulcers than horses listening to music, and racehorses stabled where a radio was played had a higher overall rate of ulceration than horses stabled where there was no radio playing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EquiNews_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-EquiNews-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>Horses have a great sense of balance, due partly to their ability to feel their footing and partly to highly developed <a href="/wiki/Proprioception" title="Proprioception">proprioception</a>—the unconscious sense of where the body and limbs are at all times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> A horse's <a href="/wiki/Somatosensory_system" title="Somatosensory system">sense of touch</a> is well-developed. The most sensitive areas are around the eyes, ears, and nose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Horses are able to sense contact as subtle as an insect landing anywhere on the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>Horses have an advanced sense of taste, which allows them to sort through <a href="/wiki/Fodder" title="Fodder">fodder</a> and choose what they would most like to eat,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> and their <a href="/wiki/Prehensility" title="Prehensility">prehensile</a> lips can easily sort even small grains. Horses generally will not eat poisonous plants, however, there are exceptions; horses will occasionally eat toxic amounts of poisonous plants even when there is adequate healthy food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Movement">Movement</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Horse_gait" title="Horse gait">Horse gait</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trot" title="Trot">Trot</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canter" title="Canter">Canter</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ambling" title="Ambling">Ambling</a></div>

</div></li>
</ul>
<p>All horses move naturally with four basic <a href="/wiki/Horse_gait" title="Horse gait">gaits</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HorseGaits32_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-HorseGaits32-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>the four-beat <a href="/wiki/Horse_gait#Walk" title="Horse gait">walk</a>, which averages 6.4 kilometres per hour (4.0 mph);</li>
<li>the two-beat <a href="/wiki/Trot" title="Trot">trot or jog</a> at 13 to 19 kilometres per hour (8.1 to 11.8 mph) (faster for <a href="/wiki/Harness_racing" title="Harness racing">harness racing</a> horses);</li>
<li>the <a href="/wiki/Canter_and_gallop" title="Canter and gallop">canter or lope</a>, a three-beat gait that is 19 to 24 kilometres per hour (12 to 15 mph);</li>
<li>the <a href="/wiki/Canter_and_gallop" title="Canter and gallop">gallop</a>, which averages 40 to 48 kilometres per hour (25 to 30 mph),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harris47_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harris47-85">[85]</a></sup> but the world record for a horse galloping over a short, sprint distance is 70.76 kilometres per hour (43.97 mph).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guinness_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-guinness-86">[86]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Besides these basic gaits, some horses perform a two-beat <a href="/wiki/Horse_gait" title="Horse gait">pace</a>, instead of the trot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HorseGaits50_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-HorseGaits50-87">[87]</a></sup> There also are several four-beat '<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ambling" title="Ambling">ambling</a>' gaits that are approximately the speed of a trot or pace, though smoother to ride. These include the lateral <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ambling#Rack" title="Ambling">rack</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ambling" title="Ambling">running walk</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T%C3%B6lt" title="Tölt">tölt</a> as well as the diagonal <a href="/wiki/Ambling_gait" title="Ambling gait">fox trot</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lieberman_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lieberman-88">[88]</a></sup> Ambling gaits are often genetic in some breeds, known collectively as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaited_horse" title="Gaited horse">gaited horses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> These horses replace the trot with one of the ambling gaits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HorseGaits51_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-HorseGaits51-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Behavior">Behavior</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Horse_behavior" title="Horse behavior">Horse behavior</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stable_vices" title="Stable vices">Stable vices</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><audio class="thumbimage" controls="" data-durationhint="3" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Wiehern.ogg" id="mwe_player_0" preload="none" style="width:220px;" width="220"><source data-bandwidth="61746" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (62 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/db/Wiehern.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'/><source data-bandwidth="96176" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-title="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/d/db/Wiehern.ogg/Wiehern.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg"/></audio> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wiehern.ogg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Horse neigh</div></div></div>
<p>Horses are prey animals with a strong <a href="/wiki/Fight-or-flight_response" title="Fight-or-flight response">fight-or-flight response</a>. Their first reaction to a threat is to startle and usually flee, although they will stand their ground and defend themselves when flight is impossible or if their young are threatened.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> They also tend to be curious; when startled, they will often hesitate an instant to ascertain the cause of their fright, and may not always flee from something that they perceive as non-threatening. Most light horse riding breeds were developed for speed, agility, alertness and endurance; natural qualities that extend from their wild ancestors. However, through selective breeding, some breeds of horses are quite docile, particularly certain draft horses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Natural226_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Natural226-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>Horses are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herd_animal" title="Herd animal">herd animals</a>, with a clear hierarchy of rank, led by a dominant individual, usually a mare. They are also social creatures that are able to form companionship attachments to their own species and to other animals, including humans. They communicate in various ways, including vocalizations such as nickering or whinnying, mutual <a href="/wiki/Social_grooming" title="Social grooming">grooming</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Body_language" title="Body language">body language</a>. Many horses will become difficult to manage if they are isolated, but with training, horses can learn to accept a human as a companion, and thus be comfortable away from other horses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ensminger305_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ensminger305-93">[93]</a></sup> However, when confined with insufficient companionship, exercise, or stimulation, individuals may develop <a href="/wiki/Stable_vices" title="Stable vices">stable vices</a>, an assortment of bad habits, mostly <a href="/wiki/Stereotypy_(non-human)" title="Stereotypy (non-human)">stereotypies</a> of psychological origin, that include wood chewing, wall kicking, "weaving" (rocking back and forth), and other problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prince214_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Prince214-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Intelligence_and_learning">Intelligence and learning</span></h4>
<p>Studies have indicated that horses perform a number of <a href="/wiki/Cognition" title="Cognition">cognitive</a> tasks on a daily basis, meeting mental challenges that include <a href="/wiki/Foraging" title="Foraging">food procurement</a> and identification of individuals within a <a href="/wiki/Social_structure" title="Social structure">social system</a>. They also have good <a href="/wiki/Spatial_visualization_ability" title="Spatial visualization ability">spatial discrimination</a> abilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hanggi_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hanggi-95">[95]</a></sup> They are naturally curious and apt to investigate things they have not seen before.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> Studies have assessed equine intelligence in areas such as <a href="/wiki/Problem_solving" title="Problem solving">problem solving</a>, speed of learning, and <a href="/wiki/Memory" title="Memory">memory</a>. Horses excel at simple learning, but also are able to use more advanced cognitive abilities that involve <a href="/wiki/Categorization" title="Categorization">categorization</a> and <a href="/wiki/Concept_learning" title="Concept learning">concept learning</a>. They can learn using <a href="/wiki/Habituation" title="Habituation">habituation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Desensitization_(psychology)" title="Desensitization (psychology)">desensitization</a>, <a href="/wiki/Classical_conditioning" title="Classical conditioning">classical conditioning</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Operant_conditioning" title="Operant conditioning">operant conditioning</a>, and positive and negative <a href="/wiki/Reinforcement" title="Reinforcement">reinforcement</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hanggi_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hanggi-95">[95]</a></sup> One study has indicated that horses can differentiate between "more or less" if the quantity involved is less than four.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-count_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-count-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>Domesticated horses may face greater mental challenges than wild horses, because they live in artificial environments that prevent <a href="/wiki/Instinct" title="Instinct">instinctive</a> behavior whilst also learning tasks that are not natural.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hanggi_95-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hanggi-95">[95]</a></sup> Horses are animals of <a href="/wiki/Habit" title="Habit">habit</a> that respond well to regimentation, and respond best when the same routines and techniques are used consistently. One trainer believes that "intelligent" horses are reflections of intelligent trainers who effectively use response conditioning techniques and positive reinforcement to train in the style that best fits with an individual animal's natural inclinations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Temperament">Temperament</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Draft_horse" title="Draft horse">Draft horse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Warmblood" title="Warmblood">Warmblood</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Oriental_horse" title="Oriental horse">Oriental horse</a></div>
<p>Horses are <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>, and as such are <a href="/wiki/Warm-blooded" title="Warm-blooded">warm-blooded</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Endotherm" title="Endotherm">endothermic</a> creatures, as opposed to cold-blooded, or <a href="/wiki/Poikilotherm" title="Poikilotherm">poikilothermic</a> animals. However, these words have developed a separate meaning in the context of equine terminology, used to describe temperament, not <a href="/wiki/Thermoregulation" title="Thermoregulation">body temperature</a>. For example, the "hot-bloods", such as many <a href="/wiki/Horse_racing" title="Horse racing">race horses</a>, exhibit more sensitivity and energy,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Belknap255_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Belknap255-99">[99]</a></sup> while the "cold-bloods", such as most <a href="/wiki/Draft_horse" title="Draft horse">draft breeds</a>, are quieter and calmer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Belknap112_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Belknap112-100">[100]</a></sup> Sometimes "hot-bloods" are classified as "light horses" or "riding horses",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ensminger71_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ensminger71-101">[101]</a></sup> with the "cold-bloods" classified as "draft horses" or "work horses".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ensminger84_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ensminger84-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brockhaus_and_Efron_Encyclopedic_Dictionary_b35_043-0.jpg"><img alt="a sepia-toned engraving from an old book, showing 11 horses of different breeds and sizes in nine different illustrations" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2545" data-file-width="3298" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Brockhaus_and_Efron_Encyclopedic_Dictionary_b35_043-0.jpg/330px-Brockhaus_and_Efron_Encyclopedic_Dictionary_b35_043-0.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Brockhaus_and_Efron_Encyclopedic_Dictionary_b35_043-0.jpg/495px-Brockhaus_and_Efron_Encyclopedic_Dictionary_b35_043-0.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Brockhaus_and_Efron_Encyclopedic_Dictionary_b35_043-0.jpg/660px-Brockhaus_and_Efron_Encyclopedic_Dictionary_b35_043-0.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brockhaus_and_Efron_Encyclopedic_Dictionary_b35_043-0.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustration of assorted breeds; slim, light hotbloods, medium-sized warmbloods and draft and pony-type coldblood breeds</div></div></div>
<p>"Hot blooded" breeds include "<a href="/wiki/Oriental_horse" title="Oriental horse">oriental horses</a>" such as the <a href="/wiki/Akhal-Teke" title="Akhal-Teke">Akhal-Teke</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arabian_horse" title="Arabian horse">Arabian horse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barb_horse" title="Barb horse">Barb</a> and now-extinct <a href="/wiki/Turkoman_horse" title="Turkoman horse">Turkoman horse</a>, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Thoroughbred" title="Thoroughbred">Thoroughbred</a>, a breed developed in England from the older oriental breeds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Belknap255_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-Belknap255-99">[99]</a></sup> Hot bloods tend to be spirited, bold, and learn quickly. They are bred for agility and speed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Catalog18_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Catalog18-103">[103]</a></sup> They tend to be physically refined—thin-skinned, slim, and long-legged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> The original oriental breeds were brought to Europe from the Middle East and North Africa when European breeders wished to infuse these traits into racing and light <a href="/wiki/Cavalry" title="Cavalry">cavalry</a> horses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whitaker43_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Whitaker43-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whitaker194_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Whitaker194-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>Muscular, heavy <a href="/wiki/Draft_horse" title="Draft horse">draft horses</a> are known as "cold bloods", as they are bred not only for strength, but also to have the calm, patient temperament needed to pull a plow or a heavy carriage full of people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Belknap112_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-Belknap112-100">[100]</a></sup> They are sometimes nicknamed "gentle giants".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Catalog15_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Catalog15-107">[107]</a></sup> Well-known draft breeds include the <a href="/wiki/Belgian_Draught" title="Belgian Draught">Belgian</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Clydesdale_horse" title="Clydesdale horse">Clydesdale</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Catalog15_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-Catalog15-107">[107]</a></sup> Some, like the <a href="/wiki/Percheron" title="Percheron">Percheron</a>, are lighter and livelier, developed to pull carriages or to plow large fields in drier climates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guide87_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guide87-108">[108]</a></sup> Others, such as the <a href="/wiki/Shire_horse" title="Shire horse">Shire</a>, are slower and more powerful, bred to plow fields with heavy, clay-based soils.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ens124_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ens124-109">[109]</a></sup> The cold-blooded group also includes some pony breeds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bennett7_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bennett7-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p><p>"<a href="/wiki/Warmblood" title="Warmblood">Warmblood</a>" breeds, such as the <a href="/wiki/Trakehner" title="Trakehner">Trakehner</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hanoverian_horse" title="Hanoverian horse">Hanoverian</a>, developed when European carriage and <a href="/wiki/Horses_in_warfare" title="Horses in warfare">war horses</a> were crossed with Arabians or Thoroughbreds, producing a riding horse with more refinement than a draft horse, but greater size and milder temperament than a lighter breed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> Certain <a href="/wiki/Pony" title="Pony">pony</a> breeds with warmblood characteristics have been developed for smaller riders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> Warmbloods are considered a "light horse" or "riding horse".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ensminger71_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ensminger71-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>Today, the term "Warmblood" refers to a specific subset of <a href="/wiki/Sport_horse" title="Sport horse">sport horse</a> breeds that are used for competition in <a href="/wiki/Dressage" title="Dressage">dressage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Show_jumping" title="Show jumping">show jumping</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lyons231_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lyons231-113">[113]</a></sup> Strictly speaking, the term "<a href="/wiki/Warmblood" title="Warmblood">warm blood</a>" refers to any <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crossbreeding" title="Crossbreeding">cross</a> between cold-blooded and hot-blooded breeds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Belknap523_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Belknap523-114">[114]</a></sup> Examples include breeds such as the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Draught" title="Irish Draught">Irish Draught</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Cleveland_Bay" title="Cleveland Bay">Cleveland Bay</a>. The term was once used to refer to breeds of light riding horse other than Thoroughbreds or Arabians, such as the <a href="/wiki/Morgan_horse" title="Morgan horse">Morgan horse</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Catalog18_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-Catalog18-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sleep_patterns">Sleep patterns</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Horse_behavior#Sleep_patterns" title="Horse behavior">Horse sleep patterns</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sleep_in_non-human_animals" title="Sleep in non-human animals">Sleep in non-humans</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Biandintz_eta_zaldiak_-_modified2.jpg"><img alt="Two horses in a pasture, one is standing beside the other that is laying down." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1282" data-file-width="1850" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Biandintz_eta_zaldiak_-_modified2.jpg/220px-Biandintz_eta_zaldiak_-_modified2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Biandintz_eta_zaldiak_-_modified2.jpg/330px-Biandintz_eta_zaldiak_-_modified2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Biandintz_eta_zaldiak_-_modified2.jpg/440px-Biandintz_eta_zaldiak_-_modified2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Biandintz_eta_zaldiak_-_modified2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>When horses lie down to sleep, others in the herd remain standing, awake or in a light doze, keeping watch.</div></div></div>
<p>Horses are able to sleep both standing up and lying down. In an adaptation from life in the wild, horses are able to enter light sleep by using a "<a href="/wiki/Stay_apparatus" title="Stay apparatus">stay apparatus</a>" in their legs, allowing them to doze without collapsing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> Horses sleep better when in groups because some animals will sleep while others stand guard to watch for predators. A horse kept alone will not sleep well because its <a href="/wiki/Instinct" title="Instinct">instincts</a> are to keep a constant eye out for danger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Horse_sleep_pt._2_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Horse_sleep_pt._2-116">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>Unlike humans, horses do not sleep in a solid, unbroken period of time, but take many short periods of rest. Horses spend four to fifteen hours a day in standing rest, and from a few minutes to several hours lying down. Total sleep time in a 24-hour period may range from several minutes to a couple of hours,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Horse_sleep_pt._2_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-Horse_sleep_pt._2-116">[116]</a></sup> mostly in short intervals of about 15 minutes each.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ensminger310_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ensminger310-117">[117]</a></sup> The average sleep time of a domestic horse is said to be 2.9 hours per day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>Horses must lie down to reach <a href="/wiki/Rapid_eye_movement_sleep" title="Rapid eye movement sleep">REM sleep</a>. They only have to lie down for an hour or two every few days to meet their minimum REM sleep requirements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Horse_sleep_pt._2_116-2"><a href="#cite_note-Horse_sleep_pt._2-116">[116]</a></sup> However, if a horse is never allowed to lie down, after several days it will become sleep-deprived, and in rare cases may suddenly collapse as it involuntarily slips into REM sleep while still standing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> This condition differs from <a href="/wiki/Narcolepsy" title="Narcolepsy">narcolepsy</a>, although horses may also suffer from that disorder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy_and_evolution">Taxonomy and evolution</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Equine_evolution.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="8223" data-file-width="9623" decoding="async" height="282" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Equine_evolution.jpg/330px-Equine_evolution.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Equine_evolution.jpg/495px-Equine_evolution.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Equine_evolution.jpg/660px-Equine_evolution.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Equine_evolution.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diagram of evolution in horses showing size development, biometrical changes in the cranium and reduction of toes (left forefoot)</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_the_horse" title="Evolution of the horse">Evolution of the horse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Equus_(genus)" title="Equus (genus)">Equus (genus)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Equidae" title="Equidae">Equidae</a></div>
<p>The horse <a href="/wiki/Adaptation" title="Adaptation">adapted</a> to survive in areas of wide-open terrain with sparse vegetation, surviving in an <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystem</a> where other large grazing animals, especially <a href="/wiki/Ruminant" title="Ruminant">ruminants</a>, could not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Budiansky_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Budiansky-121">[121]</a></sup> Horses and other equids are <a href="/wiki/Odd-toed_ungulate" title="Odd-toed ungulate">odd-toed ungulates</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Order_(biology)" title="Order (biology)">order</a> Perissodactyla, a group of mammals that was dominant during the <a href="/wiki/Tertiary" title="Tertiary">Tertiary</a> period. In the past, this order contained 14 <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">families</a>, but only three—<a href="/wiki/Equidae" title="Equidae">Equidae</a> (the horse and related species), Tapiridae (the <a href="/wiki/Tapir" title="Tapir">tapir</a>), and Rhinocerotidae (the <a href="/wiki/Rhinoceros" title="Rhinoceros">rhinoceroses</a>)—have survived to the present day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p>The earliest known member of the family Equidae was the <i><a href="/wiki/Hyracotherium" title="Hyracotherium">Hyracotherium</a></i>, which lived between 45 and 55 million years ago, during the <a href="/wiki/Eocene" title="Eocene">Eocene</a> period. It had 4 toes on each front foot, and 3 toes on each back foot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> The extra toe on the front feet soon disappeared with the <i><a href="/wiki/Mesohippus" title="Mesohippus">Mesohippus</a></i>, which lived 32 to 37 million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> Over time, the extra side toes shrank in size until they vanished. All that remains of them in modern horses is a set of small <a href="/wiki/Vestigiality" title="Vestigiality">vestigial</a> bones on the leg below the knee,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Natural_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Natural-125">[125]</a></sup> known informally as splint bones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> Their legs also lengthened as their toes disappeared until they were a hooved animal capable of running at great speed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Natural_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-Natural-125">[125]</a></sup> By about 5 million years ago, the modern <i>Equus</i> had evolved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Florida_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Florida-127">[127]</a></sup> Equid teeth also evolved from browsing on soft, tropical plants to adapt to browsing of drier plant material, then to grazing of tougher plains grasses. Thus proto-horses changed from leaf-eating forest-dwellers to grass-eating inhabitants of semi-arid regions worldwide, including the <a href="/wiki/Steppe" title="Steppe">steppes</a> of Eurasia and the <a href="/wiki/Great_Plains" title="Great Plains">Great Plains</a> of North America.
</p><p>By about 15,000 years ago, <i>Equus ferus</i> was a widespread <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holarctic" title="Holarctic">holarctic</a> species. Horse bones from this time period, the late <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a>, are found in Europe, Eurasia, <a href="/wiki/Beringia" title="Beringia">Beringia</a>, and North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weinstock_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weinstock-128">[128]</a></sup> Yet between 10,000 and 7,600 years ago, the horse became extinct in North America and rare elsewhere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VilaWidespreadOrigins_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-VilaWidespreadOrigins-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IberianOrigins_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-IberianOrigins-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haile_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-Haile-131">[131]</a></sup> The reasons for this extinction are not fully known, but one theory notes that extinction in North America paralleled human arrival.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buck_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Buck-132">[132]</a></sup> Another theory points to climate change, noting that approximately 12,500 years ago, the grasses characteristic of a steppe ecosystem gave way to shrub <a href="/wiki/Tundra" title="Tundra">tundra</a>, which was covered with unpalatable plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LeQuire_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-LeQuire-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wild_species_surviving_into_modern_times">Wild species surviving into modern times</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:France_Loz%C3%A8re_Causse_M%C3%A9jean_Chevaux_de_Przewalski_20.jpg"><img alt="Three tan-colored horses with upright manes. Two horses nip and paw at each other, while the third moves towards the camera. They stand in an open, rocky grassland, with forests in the distance." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/France_Loz%C3%A8re_Causse_M%C3%A9jean_Chevaux_de_Przewalski_20.jpg/220px-France_Loz%C3%A8re_Causse_M%C3%A9jean_Chevaux_de_Przewalski_20.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/France_Loz%C3%A8re_Causse_M%C3%A9jean_Chevaux_de_Przewalski_20.jpg/330px-France_Loz%C3%A8re_Causse_M%C3%A9jean_Chevaux_de_Przewalski_20.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/France_Loz%C3%A8re_Causse_M%C3%A9jean_Chevaux_de_Przewalski_20.jpg/440px-France_Loz%C3%A8re_Causse_M%C3%A9jean_Chevaux_de_Przewalski_20.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:France_Loz%C3%A8re_Causse_M%C3%A9jean_Chevaux_de_Przewalski_20.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A small herd of Przewalski's Horses</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wild_horse" title="Wild horse">Wild horse</a></div>
<p>A truly <a href="/wiki/Wild_horse" title="Wild horse">wild horse</a> is a species or subspecies with no ancestors that were ever domesticated. Therefore, most "wild" horses today are actually <a href="/wiki/Feral_horse" title="Feral horse">feral horses</a>, animals that escaped or were turned loose from domestic herds and the descendants of those animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Olsen46_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-Olsen46-134">[134]</a></sup> Only two wild subspecies, the <a href="/wiki/Tarpan" title="Tarpan">tarpan</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Przewalski%27s_horse" title="Przewalski's horse">Przewalski's horse</a>, survived into recorded history and only the latter survives today.
