</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Przewalski%27s_horse" title="Przewalski's horse">Przewalski's horse</a> (<i>Equus ferus przewalskii</i>), named after the Russian explorer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nikolai_Przhevalsky" title="Nikolai Przhevalsky">Nikolai Przhevalsky</a>, is a rare Asian animal. It is also known as the Mongolian wild horse; <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolian</a> people know it as the <i>taki</i>, and the <a href="/wiki/Kyrgyz_people" title="Kyrgyz people">Kyrgyz people</a> call it a <i>kirtag</i>. The subspecies was presumed extinct in the wild between 1969 and 1992, while a small breeding population survived in zoos around the world. In 1992, it was reestablished in the wild by the conservation efforts of numerous zoos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> Today, a small wild breeding population exists in Mongolia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dohner298_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dohner298-137">[137]</a></sup> There are additional animals still maintained at zoos throughout the world.
</p><p>The question of whether the Przewalski's horse was ever domesticated was challenged in 2018 when DNA studies of horses found at <a href="/wiki/Botai_culture" title="Botai culture">Botai culture</a> sites revealed captured animals with DNA markers of an ancestor to the Przewalski's horse. The study concluded that the Botai animals appear to have been an independent domestication attempt involving a different wild population from all other domesticated horses. However, the question of whether all Przewalski's horses descend from this domesticated population is unresolved, as only one of seven modern Przewalski’s horses in the study shared this ancestry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pennisi_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pennisi-138">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Orlando_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-Orlando-139">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Tarpan" title="Tarpan">tarpan</a> or European wild horse (<i>Equus ferus ferus</i>) was found in Europe and much of Asia. It survived into the historical era, but became <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">extinct</a> in 1909, when the last captive died in a Russian zoo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dohner300_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dohner300-141">[141]</a></sup> Thus, the genetic line was lost. Attempts have been made to recreate the tarpan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dohner300_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dohner300-141">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OSU_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-OSU-142">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> which resulted in horses with outward physical similarities, but nonetheless descended from domesticated ancestors and not true wild horses.
</p><p>Periodically, populations of horses in isolated areas are speculated to be <a href="/wiki/Relict_(biology)" title="Relict (biology)">relict</a> populations of wild horses, but generally have been proven to be feral or domestic. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Riwoche_horse" title="Riwoche horse">Riwoche horse</a> of Tibet was proposed as such,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dohner298_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dohner298-137">[137]</a></sup> but testing did not reveal genetic differences from domesticated horses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tibet_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tibet-144">[144]</a></sup> Similarly, the <a href="/wiki/Sorraia" title="Sorraia">Sorraia</a> of Portugal was proposed as a direct descendant of the <a href="/wiki/Tarpan" title="Tarpan">Tarpan</a> on the basis of shared characteristics,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Royo_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-Royo-145">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwards_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards-146">[146]</a></sup> but genetic studies have shown that the Sorraia is more closely related to other horse breeds and that the outward similarity is an unreliable measure of relatedness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Royo_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-Royo-145">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lira_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lira-147">[147]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_modern_equids">Other modern equids</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Equus_(genus)" title="Equus (genus)">Equus (genus)</a></div>
<p>Besides the horse, there are six other species of <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> <i>Equus</i> in the Equidae <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">family</a>. These are the ass or <a href="/wiki/Donkey" title="Donkey">donkey</a>, <i>Equus asinus</i>; the <a href="/wiki/Mountain_zebra" title="Mountain zebra">mountain zebra</a>, <i>Equus zebra</i>; <a href="/wiki/Plains_zebra" title="Plains zebra">plains zebra</a>, <i>Equus quagga</i>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gr%C3%A9vy%27s_Zebra" title="Grévy's Zebra">Grévy's zebra</a>, <i>Equus grevyi</i>; the <a href="/wiki/Kiang" title="Kiang">kiang</a>, <i>Equus kiang</i>; and the <a href="/wiki/Onager" title="Onager">onager</a>, <i>Equus hemionus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p>Horses can <a href="/wiki/Crossbreed" title="Crossbreed">crossbreed</a> with other members of their genus. The most common <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybrid</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Mule" title="Mule">mule</a>, a cross between a "jack" (male donkey) and a <a href="/wiki/Mare" title="Mare">mare</a>. A related hybrid, a <a href="/wiki/Hinny" title="Hinny">hinny</a>, is a cross between a stallion and a jenny (female donkey).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> Other hybrids include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zorse" title="Zorse">zorse</a>, a cross between a zebra and a horse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> With rare exceptions, most hybrids are <a href="/wiki/Infertility" title="Infertility">sterile</a> and cannot reproduce.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestication">Domestication</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_horse_domestication_theories" title="History of horse domestication theories">History of horse domestication theories</a> and <a href="/wiki/Domestication_of_the_horse" title="Domestication of the horse">Domestication of the horse</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bhimbetka_rock_paintng1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="578" data-file-width="906" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Bhimbetka_rock_paintng1.jpg/220px-Bhimbetka_rock_paintng1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Bhimbetka_rock_paintng1.jpg/330px-Bhimbetka_rock_paintng1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Bhimbetka_rock_paintng1.jpg/440px-Bhimbetka_rock_paintng1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bhimbetka_rock_paintng1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bhimbetka_rock_shelters" title="Bhimbetka rock shelters">Bhimbetka</a> rock painting showing a man riding on a horse, India</div></div></div>
<p>Domestication of the horse most likely took place in central Asia prior to 3500 BC. Two major sources of information are used to determine where and when the horse was first domesticated and how the domesticated horse spread around the world. The first source is based on <a href="/wiki/Paleontology" title="Paleontology">palaeological</a> and archaeological discoveries; the second source is a comparison of DNA obtained from modern horses to that from bones and teeth of ancient horse remains.
</p><p>The earliest archaeological evidence for the <a href="/wiki/Domestication_of_the_horse" title="Domestication of the horse">domestication of the horse</a> comes from sites in <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>, dating to approximately 4000–3500 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> By 3000 BC, the horse was completely domesticated and by 2000 BC there was a sharp increase in the number of horse bones found in human settlements in northwestern Europe, indicating the spread of domesticated horses throughout the continent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> The most recent, but most irrefutable evidence of domestication comes from sites where horse remains were interred with chariots in graves of the <a href="/wiki/Sintashta" title="Sintashta">Sintashta</a> and <a href="/wiki/Petrovka_settlement" title="Petrovka settlement">Petrovka</a> cultures c. 2100 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuznetsov2006_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kuznetsov2006-156">[156]</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2021 genetic study suggested that most modern domestic horses descend from the lower <a href="/wiki/Volga_region" title="Volga region">Volga-Don region</a>. <a href="/wiki/Ancient_DNA" title="Ancient DNA">Ancient horse genomes</a> indicate that these populations influenced almost all local populations as they expanded rapidly throughout Eurasia, beginning about 4200 years ago. It also shows that certain adaptations were strongly selected due to <a href="/wiki/Equestrianism" title="Equestrianism">riding</a>, and that equestrian material culture, including <a href="/wiki/Sintashta_culture" title="Sintashta culture">Sintashta</a> spoke-wheeled <a href="/wiki/Chariot" title="Chariot">chariots</a> spread with the horse itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup>
</p><p>Domestication is also studied by using the genetic material of present-day horses and comparing it with the genetic material present in the bones and teeth of horse remains found in archaeological and palaeological excavations. The variation in the genetic material shows that very few wild stallions contributed to the domestic horse,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lau_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lau-159">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lindgren2004_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lindgren2004-160">[160]</a></sup> while many mares were part of early domesticated herds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lira_147-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lira-147">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vila2001_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vila2001-161">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cai_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cai-162">[162]</a></sup> This is reflected in the difference in genetic variation between the DNA that is passed on along the paternal, or sire line (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Y-chromosome" title="Y-chromosome">Y-chromosome</a>) versus that passed on along the maternal, or dam line (<a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial DNA</a>). There are very low levels of Y-chromosome variability,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lau_159-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lau-159">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lindgren2004_160-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lindgren2004-160">[160]</a></sup> but a great deal of genetic variation in mitochondrial DNA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lira_147-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lira-147">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vila2001_161-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vila2001-161">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cai_162-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cai-162">[162]</a></sup> There is also regional variation in mitochondrial DNA due to the inclusion of wild mares in domestic herds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lira_147-3"><a href="#cite_note-Lira-147">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vila2001_161-2"><a href="#cite_note-Vila2001-161">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cai_162-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cai-162">[162]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> Another characteristic of domestication is an increase in coat color variation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> In horses, this increased dramatically between 5000 and 3000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coatColor_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-coatColor-165">[165]</a></sup>
</p><p>Before the availability of DNA techniques to resolve the questions related to the domestication of the horse, various hypotheses were proposed. One classification was based on body types and conformation, suggesting the presence of four basic prototypes that had adapted to their environment prior to domestication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bennett7_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bennett7-110">[110]</a></sup> Another hypothesis held that the four prototypes originated from a single wild species and that all different body types were entirely a result of <a href="/wiki/Selective_breeding" title="Selective breeding">selective breeding</a> after domestication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> However, the lack of a detectable substructure  in the horse has resulted in a rejection of both hypotheses.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feral_populations">Feral populations</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Feral_horse" title="Feral horse">Feral horse</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Feral" title="Feral">Feral</a> horses are born and live in the wild, but are descended from domesticated animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Olsen46_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-Olsen46-134">[134]</a></sup> Many populations of <a href="/wiki/Feral_horse" title="Feral horse">feral horses</a> exist throughout the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> Studies of feral herds have provided useful insights into the behavior of prehistoric horses,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> as well as greater understanding of the instincts and behaviors that drive horses that live in domesticated conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are also <a href="/wiki/Semi-feral" title="Semi-feral">semi-feral</a> horses in many parts of the world, such as <a href="/wiki/Dartmoor" title="Dartmoor">Dartmoor</a> and the <a href="/wiki/New_Forest" title="New Forest">New Forest</a> in the UK, where the animals are all privately owned but live for significant amounts of time in "wild" conditions on undeveloped, often public, lands. Owners of such animals often pay a fee for grazing rights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fear75_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fear75-172">[172]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeds">Breeds</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Horse_breed" title="Horse breed">Horse breed</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_horse_breeds" title="List of horse breeds">List of horse breeds</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Horse_breeding" title="Horse breeding">Horse breeding</a></div>
<p>The concept of <a href="/wiki/Purebred" title="Purebred">purebred</a> bloodstock and a controlled, written <a href="/wiki/Breed_registry" title="Breed registry">breed registry</a> has come to be particularly significant and important in modern times. Sometimes purebred horses are incorrectly or inaccurately called "thoroughbreds". <a href="/wiki/Thoroughbred" title="Thoroughbred">Thoroughbred</a> is a specific breed of horse, while a "purebred" is a horse (or any other animal) with a defined <a href="/wiki/Pedigree_chart" title="Pedigree chart">pedigree</a> recognized by a breed registry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ensminger424_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ensminger424-173">[173]</a></sup> Horse breeds are groups of horses with distinctive characteristics that are transmitted consistently to their offspring, such as <a href="/wiki/Equine_conformation" title="Equine conformation">conformation</a>, color, performance ability, or disposition. These inherited traits result from a combination of natural crosses and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artificial_selection" title="Artificial selection">artificial selection</a> methods. Horses have been <a href="/wiki/Selective_breeding" title="Selective breeding">selectively bred</a> since their <a href="/wiki/Domestication_of_the_horse" title="Domestication of the horse">domestication</a>. An early example of people who practiced selective <a href="/wiki/Horse_breeding" title="Horse breeding">horse breeding</a> were the <a href="/wiki/Bedouin" title="Bedouin">Bedouin</a>, who had a reputation for careful practices, keeping extensive pedigrees of their <a href="/wiki/Arabian_horse" title="Arabian horse">Arabian horses</a> and placing great value upon pure bloodlines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> These pedigrees were originally transmitted via an <a href="/wiki/Oral_tradition" title="Oral tradition">oral tradition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> In the 14th century, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carthusian" title="Carthusian">Carthusian</a> monks of southern Spain kept meticulous pedigrees of bloodstock lineages still found today in the <a href="/wiki/Andalusian_horse" title="Andalusian horse">Andalusian horse</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup>
</p><p>Breeds developed due to a need for "form to function", the necessity to develop certain characteristics in order to perform a particular type of work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sponenberg155_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sponenberg155-177">[177]</a></sup> Thus, a powerful but refined breed such as the Andalusian developed as riding horses with an aptitude for <a href="/wiki/Dressage" title="Dressage">dressage</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sponenberg155_177-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sponenberg155-177">[177]</a></sup> Heavy draft horses were developed out of a need to perform demanding <a href="/wiki/Farm" title="Farm">farm</a> work and pull heavy wagons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> Other horse breeds had been developed specifically for light agricultural work, carriage and road work, various sport disciplines, or simply as pets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spon162_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spon162-179">[179]</a></sup> Some breeds developed through centuries of crossing other breeds, while others descended from a single <a href="/wiki/Foundation_stock" title="Foundation stock">foundation sire</a>, or other limited or restricted foundation bloodstock. One of the earliest formal registries was <a href="/wiki/General_Stud_Book" title="General Stud Book">General Stud Book</a> for Thoroughbreds, which began in 1791 and traced back to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Foundation_bloodstock" title="Foundation bloodstock">foundation bloodstock</a> for the breed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> There are more than 300 horse breeds in the world today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Interaction_with_humans">Interaction with humans</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Murron-Ryhti_pulling.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1745" data-file-width="2063" decoding="async" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Murron-Ryhti_pulling.png/220px-Murron-Ryhti_pulling.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Murron-Ryhti_pulling.png/330px-Murron-Ryhti_pulling.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Murron-Ryhti_pulling.png/440px-Murron-Ryhti_pulling.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Murron-Ryhti_pulling.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Finnhorse" title="Finnhorse">Finnhorse</a> pulling a heavy wagon.</div></div></div>
<p>Worldwide, horses play a role within human cultures and have done so for millennia. Horses are used for leisure activities, sports, and working purposes. The <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">Food and Agriculture Organization</a> (FAO) estimates that in 2008, there were almost 59,000,000 horses in the world, with around 33,500,000 in the Americas, 13,800,000 in Asia and 6,300,000 in Europe and smaller portions in Africa and Oceania. There are estimated to be 9,500,000 horses in the United States alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/American_Horse_Council" title="American Horse Council">American Horse Council</a> estimates that horse-related activities have a direct impact on the economy of the United States of over $39 billion, and when indirect spending is considered, the impact is over $102 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup> In a 2004 "poll" conducted by <a href="/wiki/Animal_Planet" title="Animal Planet">Animal Planet</a>, more than 50,000 viewers from 73 countries voted for the horse as the world's 4th favorite animal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IOL_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-IOL-184">[184]</a></sup>
</p><p>Communication between human and horse is paramount in any equestrian activity;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Olsen_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-Olsen-185">[185]</a></sup> to aid this process horses are usually ridden with a <a href="/wiki/Saddle" title="Saddle">saddle</a> on their backs to assist the rider with balance and positioning, and a <a href="/wiki/Bridle" title="Bridle">bridle</a> or related headgear to assist the rider in maintaining control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> Sometimes horses are ridden without a saddle,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup> and occasionally, horses are trained to perform without a bridle or other headgear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> Many horses are also <a href="/wiki/Driving_(horse)" title="Driving (horse)">driven</a>, which requires a <a href="/wiki/Horse_harness" title="Horse harness">harness</a>, bridle, and some type of <a href="/wiki/Horse-drawn_vehicle" title="Horse-drawn vehicle">vehicle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sport">Sport</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beijing2008_HOKETSU_Hiroshi.JPG"><img alt="A chestnut (reddish-brown) horse being ridden by a rider in a black coat and top hat. They are stopped in a riding arena with the rider tipping his hat." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="442" data-file-width="547" decoding="async" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Beijing2008_HOKETSU_Hiroshi.JPG/260px-Beijing2008_HOKETSU_Hiroshi.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Beijing2008_HOKETSU_Hiroshi.JPG/390px-Beijing2008_HOKETSU_Hiroshi.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Beijing2008_HOKETSU_Hiroshi.JPG/520px-Beijing2008_HOKETSU_Hiroshi.JPG 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Beijing2008_HOKETSU_Hiroshi.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A horse and rider in <a href="/wiki/Dressage" title="Dressage">dressage</a> competition at the <a href="/wiki/Equestrian_at_the_Summer_Olympics" title="Equestrian at the Summer Olympics">Olympics</a></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Equestrianism" title="Equestrianism">Equestrianism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Horse_racing" title="Horse racing">Horse racing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Horse_training" title="Horse training">Horse training</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Horse_tack" title="Horse tack">Horse tack</a></div>
<p>Historically, equestrians honed their skills through games and races. Equestrian sports provided entertainment for crowds and honed the excellent horsemanship that was needed in battle. Many sports, such as <a href="/wiki/Dressage" title="Dressage">dressage</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eventing" title="Eventing">eventing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Show_jumping" title="Show jumping">show jumping</a>, have origins in <a href="/wiki/Horses_in_warfare" title="Horses in warfare">military training</a>, which were focused on control and balance of both horse and rider. Other sports, such as <a href="/wiki/Rodeo" title="Rodeo">rodeo</a>, developed from practical skills such as those needed on working <a href="/wiki/Ranch" title="Ranch">ranches</a> and <a href="/wiki/Station_(Australian_agriculture)" title="Station (Australian agriculture)">stations</a>. Sport hunting from horseback evolved from earlier practical hunting techniques.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Olsen_185-1"><a href="#cite_note-Olsen-185">[185]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Horse_racing" title="Horse racing">Horse racing</a> of all types evolved from impromptu competitions between riders or drivers. All forms of competition, requiring demanding and specialized skills from both horse and rider, resulted in the systematic development of specialized breeds and equipment for each sport. The popularity of equestrian sports through the centuries has resulted in the preservation of skills that would otherwise have disappeared after horses stopped being used in combat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Olsen_185-2"><a href="#cite_note-Olsen-185">[185]</a></sup>
</p><p>Horses are trained to be ridden or driven in a variety of sporting competitions. Examples include <a href="/wiki/Show_jumping" title="Show jumping">show jumping</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dressage" title="Dressage">dressage</a>, three-day <a href="/wiki/Eventing" title="Eventing">eventing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Combined_driving" title="Combined driving">competitive driving</a>, <a href="/wiki/Endurance_riding" title="Endurance riding">endurance riding</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gymkhana_(equestrian)" title="Gymkhana (equestrian)">gymkhana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rodeo" title="Rodeo">rodeos</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fox_hunting" title="Fox hunting">fox hunting</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Horse_show" title="Horse show">Horse shows</a>, which have their origins in medieval European fairs, are held around the world. They host a huge range of classes, covering all of the mounted and harness disciplines, as well as <a href="/wiki/Halter_(horse_show)" title="Halter (horse show)">"In-hand"</a> classes where the horses are led, rather than ridden, to be evaluated on their conformation. The method of judging varies with the discipline, but winning usually depends on style and ability of both horse and rider.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup>
Sports such as <a href="/wiki/Polo" title="Polo">polo</a> do not judge the horse itself, but rather use the horse as a partner for human competitors as a necessary part of the game. Although the horse requires specialized training to participate, the details of its performance are not judged, only the result of the rider's actions—be it getting a ball through a goal or some other task.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwards360_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards360-192">[192]</a></sup> Examples of these sports of partnership between human and horse include <a href="/wiki/Jousting" title="Jousting">jousting</a>, in which the main goal is for one rider to unseat the other,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Buzkashi" title="Buzkashi">buzkashi</a>, a team game played throughout <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>, the aim being to capture a goat carcass while on horseback.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwards360_192-1"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards360-192">[192]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Horse_racing" title="Horse racing">Horse racing</a> is an equestrian sport and major international industry, watched in almost every nation of the world. There are three types: "flat" racing; <a href="/wiki/Steeplechase_(horse_racing)" title="Steeplechase (horse racing)">steeplechasing</a>, i.e. racing over jumps; and <a href="/wiki/Harness_racing" title="Harness racing">harness racing</a>, where horses trot or pace while pulling a driver in a small, light cart known as a <a href="/wiki/Sulky" title="Sulky">sulky</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup> A major part of horse racing's economic importance lies in the <a href="/wiki/Gambling#Parimutuel_betting" title="Gambling">gambling</a> associated with it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Work">Work</span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:392px;max-width:392px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:253px;max-width:253px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:167px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tanga_(carriage)_at_Darbhanga_Bihar.jpg"><img alt="Tired-looking bay horse hitched to a rustic cart" data-file-height="576" data-file-width="864" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Tanga_%28carriage%29_at_Darbhanga_Bihar.jpg/251px-Tanga_%28carriage%29_at_Darbhanga_Bihar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Tanga_%28carriage%29_at_Darbhanga_Bihar.jpg/377px-Tanga_%28carriage%29_at_Darbhanga_Bihar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Tanga_%28carriage%29_at_Darbhanga_Bihar.jpg/502px-Tanga_%28carriage%29_at_Darbhanga_Bihar.jpg 2x" width="251"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Horse pulling a cart</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:135px;max-width:135px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:167px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Policja_konna_Pozna%C5%84.jpg"><img alt="A mounted man in a blue uniform on a dark brown horse" data-file-height="783" data-file-width="619" decoding="async" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Policja_konna_Pozna%C5%84.jpg/133px-Policja_konna_Pozna%C5%84.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Policja_konna_Pozna%C5%84.jpg/200px-Policja_konna_Pozna%C5%84.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Policja_konna_Pozna%C5%84.jpg/266px-Policja_konna_Pozna%C5%84.jpg 2x" width="133"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">A mounted police officer in Poland</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>There are certain jobs that horses do very well, and no technology has yet developed to fully replace them. For example, <a href="/wiki/Mounted_police" title="Mounted police">mounted police</a> horses are still effective for certain types of patrol duties and crowd control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup> Cattle <a href="/wiki/Ranch" title="Ranch">ranches</a> still require riders on horseback to round up cattle that are scattered across remote, rugged terrain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Search_and_rescue" title="Search and rescue">Search and rescue</a> organizations in some countries depend upon <a href="/wiki/Mounted_search_and_rescue" title="Mounted search and rescue">mounted</a> teams to locate people, particularly hikers and children, and to provide disaster relief assistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup> Horses can also be used in areas where it is necessary to avoid vehicular disruption to delicate soil, such as nature reserves. They may also be the only form of transport allowed in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wilderness_area" title="Wilderness area">wilderness areas</a>. Horses are quieter than motorized vehicles. <a href="/wiki/Law_enforcement_officer" title="Law enforcement officer">Law enforcement officers</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Park_ranger" title="Park ranger">park rangers</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Game_warden" title="Game warden">game wardens</a> may use horses for patrols, and horses or mules may also be used for clearing trails or other work in areas of rough terrain where vehicles are less effective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although machinery has replaced horses in many parts of the world, an estimated 100 million horses, donkeys and mules are still used for agriculture and transportation in less developed areas. This number includes around 27 million <a href="/wiki/Working_animal" title="Working animal">working animals</a> in Africa alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup> Some land management practices such as cultivating and logging can be efficiently performed with horses. In agriculture, less fossil fuel is used and increased environmental conservation occurs over time with the use of <a href="/wiki/Working_animal" title="Working animal">draft animals</a> such as horses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup> Logging with horses can result in reduced damage to soil structure and less damage to trees due to more selective logging.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Warfare">Warfare</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Horses_in_warfare" title="Horses in warfare">Horses in warfare</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1970-073-17,_T%C3%BCrkische_Kavallerie_s%C3%BCdlich_von_Jerusalem.jpg"><img alt="Black-and-white photo of mounted soldiers with middle eastern headwraps, carrying rifles, walking down a road away from the camera" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="553" data-file-width="789" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1970-073-17%2C_T%C3%BCrkische_Kavallerie_s%C3%BCdlich_von_Jerusalem.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1970-073-17%2C_T%C3%BCrkische_Kavallerie_s%C3%BCdlich_von_Jerusalem.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1970-073-17%2C_T%C3%BCrkische_Kavallerie_s%C3%BCdlich_von_Jerusalem.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1970-073-17%2C_T%C3%BCrkische_Kavallerie_s%C3%BCdlich_von_Jerusalem.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1970-073-17%2C_T%C3%BCrkische_Kavallerie_s%C3%BCdlich_von_Jerusalem.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1970-073-17%2C_T%C3%BCrkische_Kavallerie_s%C3%BCdlich_von_Jerusalem.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1970-073-17,_T%C3%BCrkische_Kavallerie_s%C3%BCdlich_von_Jerusalem.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> cavalry, 1917</div></div></div>
<p>Horses have been used in warfare for most of recorded history. The first archaeological evidence of horses used in warfare dates to between 4000 and 3000 BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Science_Show_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-Science_Show-204">[204]</a></sup> and the use of horses in warfare was widespread by the end of the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hartwick_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hartwick-205">[205]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whitaker30_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-Whitaker30-206">[206]</a></sup> Although mechanization has largely replaced the horse as a weapon of war, horses are still seen today in limited military uses, mostly for ceremonial purposes, or for reconnaissance and transport activities in areas of rough terrain where motorized vehicles are ineffective. Horses have been used in the 21st century by the <a href="/wiki/Janjaweed" title="Janjaweed">Janjaweed</a> militias in the <a href="/wiki/War_in_Darfur" title="War in Darfur">War in Darfur</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Entertainment_and_culture">Entertainment and culture</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hayagreeva.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="683" data-file-width="487" decoding="async" height="238" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Hayagreeva.jpg/170px-Hayagreeva.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Hayagreeva.jpg/255px-Hayagreeva.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Hayagreeva.jpg/340px-Hayagreeva.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hayagreeva.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The horse-headed deity in Hinduism, <a href="/wiki/Hayagriva" title="Hayagriva">Hayagriva</a></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Horses_in_art" title="Horses in art">Horses in art</a> and <a href="/wiki/Horse_worship" title="Horse worship">Horse worship</a></div>
<p>Modern horses are often used to reenact many of their historical work purposes. Horses are used, complete with equipment that is authentic or a meticulously recreated replica, in various live action <a href="/wiki/Historical_reenactment" title="Historical reenactment">historical reenactments</a> of specific periods of history, especially recreations of famous battles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup> Horses are also used to preserve cultural traditions and for ceremonial purposes. Countries such as the United Kingdom still use horse-drawn carriages to convey royalty and other VIPs to and from certain culturally significant events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup> Public exhibitions are another example, such as the <a href="/wiki/Budweiser_Clydesdales" title="Budweiser Clydesdales">Budweiser Clydesdales</a>, seen in parades and other public settings, a team of <a href="/wiki/Draft_horse" title="Draft horse">draft horses</a> that pull a beer wagon similar to that used before the invention of the modern motorized truck.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup>
</p><p>Horses are frequently used in television, films and literature. They are sometimes featured as a major character in films about particular animals, but also used as visual elements that assure the accuracy of historical stories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup> Both live horses and <a href="/wiki/Cultural_icon" title="Cultural icon">iconic</a> images of horses are used in <a href="/wiki/Advertising" title="Advertising">advertising</a> to promote a variety of products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup> The horse frequently appears in coats of arms in <a href="/wiki/Heraldry" title="Heraldry">heraldry</a>, in a variety of poses and equipment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mythology" title="Mythology">mythologies</a> of many cultures, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greco-Roman_mythology" title="Greco-Roman mythology">Greco-Roman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hindu_mythology" title="Hindu mythology">Hindu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islamic_mythology" title="Islamic mythology">Islamic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Norse_mythology" title="Norse mythology">Norse</a>, include references to both normal horses and those with wings or additional limbs, and multiple myths also call upon the horse to draw the chariots of the Moon and Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup> The horse also appears in the 12-year cycle of animals in the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_astrology" title="Chinese astrology">Chinese zodiac</a> related to the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_calendar" title="Chinese calendar">Chinese calendar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup> Horses serve as the inspiration for many modern automobile names and logos, including the <a href="/wiki/Ford_Pinto" title="Ford Pinto">Ford Pinto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ford_Bronco" title="Ford Bronco">Ford Bronco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ford_Mustang" title="Ford Mustang">Ford Mustang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hyundai_Equus" title="Hyundai Equus">Hyundai Equus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hyundai_Pony" title="Hyundai Pony">Hyundai Pony</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi_Starion" title="Mitsubishi Starion">Mitsubishi Starion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Subaru_BRAT" title="Subaru BRAT">Subaru Brumby</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi_Colt" title="Mitsubishi Colt">Mitsubishi Colt</a>/<a href="/wiki/Dodge_Colt" title="Dodge Colt">Dodge Colt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Volkswagen_Polo" title="Volkswagen Polo">Volkswagen Polo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Steyr-Puch_Pinzgauer" title="Steyr-Puch Pinzgauer">Pinzgauer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Steyr-Puch_Haflinger" title="Steyr-Puch Haflinger">Steyr-Puch Haflinger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pegaso" title="Pegaso">Pegaso</a>, <a href="/wiki/Porsche" title="Porsche">Porsche</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Camargue" title="Rolls-Royce Camargue">Rolls-Royce Camargue</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ferrari" title="Ferrari">Ferrari</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carlsson_(car_company)" title="Carlsson (car company)">Carlsson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kamaz" title="Kamaz">Kamaz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Corre_La_Licorne" title="Corre La Licorne">Corre La Licorne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iran_Khodro" title="Iran Khodro">Iran Khodro</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eicher_Motors" title="Eicher Motors">Eicher</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Baojun" title="Baojun">Baojun</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Therapeutic_use">Therapeutic use</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Equine-assisted_therapy" title="Equine-assisted therapy">Equine-assisted therapy</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Therapeutic_horseback_riding" title="Therapeutic horseback riding">Therapeutic horseback riding</a></div>
<p>People of all ages with physical and mental disabilities obtain beneficial results from an association with horses. Therapeutic riding is used to mentally and physically stimulate disabled persons and help them improve their lives through improved balance and coordination, increased self-confidence, and a greater feeling of freedom and independence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup> The benefits of equestrian activity for people with disabilities has also been recognized with the addition of equestrian events to the <a href="/wiki/Paralympic_Games" title="Paralympic Games">Paralympic Games</a> and recognition of <a href="/wiki/Para-equestrian" title="Para-equestrian">para-equestrian</a> events by the <a href="/wiki/International_Federation_for_Equestrian_Sports" title="International Federation for Equestrian Sports">International Federation for Equestrian Sports</a> (FEI).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup> Hippotherapy and therapeutic horseback riding are names for different physical, occupational, and speech therapy treatment strategies that utilize equine movement. In hippotherapy, a therapist uses the horse's movement to improve their patient's cognitive, coordination, balance, and fine motor skills, whereas therapeutic horseback riding uses specific riding skills.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup>
</p><p>Horses also provide psychological benefits to people whether they actually ride or not. "Equine-assisted" or "equine-facilitated" therapy is a form of experiential <a href="/wiki/Psychotherapy" title="Psychotherapy">psychotherapy</a> that uses horses as companion animals to assist people with mental illness, including anxiety disorders, psychotic disorders, mood disorders, behavioral difficulties, and those who are going through major life changes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup> There are also experimental programs using horses in <a href="/wiki/Prison" title="Prison">prison</a> settings. Exposure to horses appears to improve the behavior of inmates and help reduce <a href="/wiki/Recidivism" title="Recidivism">recidivism</a> when they leave.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Products">Products</span></h3>
<p>Horses are raw material for many products made by humans throughout history, including byproducts from the <a href="/wiki/Horse_slaughter" title="Horse slaughter">slaughter</a> of horses as well as materials collected from living horses.
</p><p>Products collected from living horses include mare's milk, used by people with large horse herds, such as the <a href="/wiki/Mongols" title="Mongols">Mongols</a>, who let it ferment to produce <a href="/wiki/Kumis" title="Kumis">kumis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NewYorker_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorker-224">[224]</a></sup> Horse blood was once used as food by the Mongols and other <a href="/wiki/Nomad" title="Nomad">nomadic</a> tribes, who found it a convenient source of nutrition when traveling. Drinking their own horses' blood allowed the Mongols to ride for extended periods of time without stopping to eat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NewYorker_224-1"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorker-224">[224]</a></sup> The drug <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Premarin" title="Premarin">Premarin</a> is a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Estrogen" title="Estrogen">estrogens</a> extracted from the urine of pregnant mares (<b>pre</b>gnant <b>mar</b>es' ur<b>in</b>e), and was previously a widely used drug for <a href="/wiki/Hormone_replacement_therapy" title="Hormone replacement therapy">hormone replacement therapy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup> The tail hair of horses can be used for making <a href="/wiki/Bow_(music)" title="Bow (music)">bows</a> for <a href="/wiki/String_instrument" title="String instrument">string instruments</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Violin" title="Violin">violin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Viola" title="Viola">viola</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cello" title="Cello">cello</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Double_bass" title="Double bass">double bass</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Horse_meat" title="Horse meat">Horse meat</a> has been used as food for humans and <a href="/wiki/Carnivore" title="Carnivore">carnivorous animals</a> throughout the ages. Approximately 5 million horses are slaughtered each year for meat worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup> It is eaten in many parts of the world, though consumption is <a href="/wiki/Taboo" title="Taboo">taboo</a> in some cultures,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USDA_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-USDA-228">[228]</a></sup> and a subject of political controversy in others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[229]</a></sup> Horsehide leather has been used for boots, gloves, <a href="/wiki/A-2_jacket" title="A-2 jacket">jackets</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-by-product_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-by-product-230">[230]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Baseball_(ball)" title="Baseball (ball)">baseballs</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup> and baseball gloves. Horse hooves can also be used to produce <a href="/wiki/Animal_glue" title="Animal glue">animal glue</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup> Horse bones can be used to make implements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[233]</a></sup> Specifically, in Italian cuisine, the horse <a href="/wiki/Tibia" title="Tibia">tibia</a> is sharpened into a probe called a <i>spinto</i>, which is used to test the readiness of a (pig) ham as it cures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[234]</a></sup> In Asia, the saba is a horsehide vessel used in the production of <a href="/wiki/Kumis" title="Kumis">kumis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[235]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Care">Care</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horse_care" title="Horse care">Horse care</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Equine_nutrition" title="Equine nutrition">Equine nutrition</a>, <a href="/wiki/Horse_grooming" title="Horse grooming">Horse grooming</a>, <a href="/wiki/Veterinary_medicine" title="Veterinary medicine">Veterinary medicine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Farrier" title="Farrier">Farrier</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Army.mil-2008-04-18-100048.jpg"><img alt="A young man in US military clothing examines the teeth of a bay (dark brown) horse, while another person in military work clothing, partially obscured, holds the horse. Several other people are partially visible in the background." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2286" data-file-width="3522" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Army.mil-2008-04-18-100048.jpg/220px-Army.mil-2008-04-18-100048.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Army.mil-2008-04-18-100048.jpg/330px-Army.mil-2008-04-18-100048.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Army.mil-2008-04-18-100048.jpg/440px-Army.mil-2008-04-18-100048.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Army.mil-2008-04-18-100048.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Checking teeth and other physical examinations are an important part of horse care.</div></div></div>
<p>Horses are <a href="/wiki/Grazing" title="Grazing">grazing</a> animals, and their major source of nutrients is good-quality <a href="/wiki/Forage" title="Forage">forage</a> from <a href="/wiki/Hay" title="Hay">hay</a> or <a href="/wiki/Pasture" title="Pasture">pasture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup> They can consume approximately 2% to 2.5% of their body weight in dry feed each day. Therefore, a 450-kilogram (990 lb) adult horse could eat up to 11 kilograms (24 lb) of food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Penn_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-Penn-237">[237]</a></sup> Sometimes, concentrated feed such as <a href="/wiki/Cereal" title="Cereal">grain</a> is fed in addition to pasture or hay, especially when the animal is very active.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[238]</a></sup> When grain is fed, equine nutritionists recommend that 50% or more of the animal's diet by weight should still be forage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feeding_factors_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-Feeding_factors-239">[239]</a></sup>
</p><p>Horses require a plentiful supply of clean water, a minimum of 10 US gallons (38 L) to 12 US gallons (45 L) per day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[240]</a></sup> Although horses are adapted to live outside, they require shelter from the wind and <a href="/wiki/Precipitation" title="Precipitation">precipitation</a>, which can range from a simple shed or shelter to an elaborate <a href="/wiki/Stable" title="Stable">stable</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[241]</a></sup>
</p><p>Horses require routine <a href="/wiki/Horse_hoof" title="Horse hoof">hoof</a> care from a <a href="/wiki/Farrier" title="Farrier">farrier</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Vaccination" title="Vaccination">vaccinations</a> to protect against various diseases, and <a href="/wiki/Horse_teeth" title="Horse teeth">dental</a> examinations from a <a href="/wiki/Veterinarian" title="Veterinarian">veterinarian</a> or a specialized equine dentist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[242]</a></sup> If horses are kept inside in a barn, they require regular daily exercise for their physical health and mental well-being.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[243]</a></sup> When turned outside, they require well-maintained, sturdy <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_fencing" title="Agricultural fencing">fences</a> to be safely contained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[244]</a></sup> Regular <a href="/wiki/Horse_grooming" title="Horse grooming">grooming</a> is also helpful to help the horse maintain good health of the hair coat and underlying skin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[245]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
